Each key maps to a value, allowing you to store and retrieve data using meaningful names instead of numeric indices. Keys must be unique and immutable (like strings, numbers, or tuples).</p>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Creating Dictionaries</h2>\n                <p>Create dictionaries using curly braces {} with key:value pairs, or the dict() constructor.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Dictionary Creation:</h4>\n<pre><code># Empty dictionary\nempty = {}\nalso_empty = dict()\n\n# Dictionary with initial values\nperson = {\n    "name": "Alice",\n    "age": 25,\n    "city": "New York"\n}\n\n# Using dict() constructor\nperson2 = dict(name="Bob", age=30, city="LA")\n\n# From list of tuples\nitems = [("a", 1), ("b", 2)]\nd = dict(items)  # {\'a\': 1, \'b\': 2}\n\n# Dictionary with different key types\nmixed_keys = {\n    "string": "value",\n    42: "number key",\n    (1, 2): "tuple key"\n}\n\n# Nested dictionaries\ncompany = {\n    "name": "TechCorp",\n    "employees": {\n        "count": 50,\n        "departments": ["Engineering", "Sales", "HR"]\n    }\n}\n\n# Dictionary comprehension\nsquares = {x: x**2 for x in range(1, 6)}\nprint(squares)  # {1: 1, 2: 4, 3: 9, 4: 16, 5: 25}</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-3:</strong> Two ways to create empty dictionaries.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 6-10:</strong> Curly braces with key:value pairs is the most common syntax.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-14:</strong> The dict() constructor can take keyword arguments.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-22:</strong> Keys can be any immutable type: strings, numbers, tuples.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-30:</strong> Dictionaries can contain other dictionaries as values.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 33-34:</strong> Dictionary comprehension creates dictionaries from iterables.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Accessing Dictionary Values</h2>\n                <p>Access values using keys in square brackets or the get() method for safer access.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Value Access:</h4>\n<pre><code>person = {"name": "Alice", "age": 25, "city": "NYC"}\n\n# Access by key\nprint(person["name"])  # Alice\nprint(person["age"])   # 25\n\n# KeyError if key doesn\'t exist\n# print(person["email"])  # KeyError!\n\n# Safe access with get()\nprint(person.get("name"))      # Alice\nprint(person.get("email"))     # None (no error)\nprint(person.get("email", "N/A"))  # N/A (default value)\n\n# Check if key exists\nprint("name" in person)   # True\nprint("email" in person)  # False\n\n# Access nested values\ncompany = {\n    "name": "TechCorp",\n    "employees": {"count": 50}\n}\nprint(company["employees"]["count"])  # 50\n\n# Using .keys(), .values(), .items()\nprint(person.keys())    # dict_keys([\'name\', \'age\', \'city\'])\nprint(person.values())  # dict_values([\'Alice\', 25, \'NYC\'])\nprint(person.items())   # dict_items([(\'name\', \'Alice\'), ...])\n\n# Convert to lists\nkeys_list = list(person.keys())\nvalues_list = list(person.values())</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-5:</strong> Use square brackets with key name to access values.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Line 8:</strong> Missing keys cause KeyError when using bracket notation.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-13:</strong> <code>get()</code> returns None (or a default) for missing keys.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 16-17:</strong> Use <code>in</code> to check if a key exists.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-25:</strong> Access nested dictionary values with chained brackets.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 28-33:</strong> View methods return all keys, values, or key-value pairs.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Modifying Dictionaries</h2>\n                <p>Dictionaries are mutable. You can add, update, and delete key-value pairs.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Dictionary Modification:</h4>\n<pre><code>person = {"name": "Alice", "age": 25}\n\n# Add or update by assignment\nperson["city"] = "NYC"      # Add new key\nperson["age"] = 26          # Update existing\nprint(person)  # {\'name\': \'Alice\', \'age\': 26, \'city\': \'NYC\'}\n\n# Update multiple pairs\nperson.update({"email": "alice@example.com", "age": 27})\nprint(person)\n\n# Update from keyword arguments\nperson.update(country="USA", phone="555-1234")\n\n# Remove items\nremoved = person.pop("phone")  # Remove and return value\nprint(removed)  # 555-1234\n\n# Pop with default (avoids KeyError)\nemail = person.pop("email", "no email")\n\n# Remove last inserted (Python 3.7+)\nlast = person.popitem()\nprint(last)  # (\'city\', \'NYC\')\n\n# Delete key\ndel person["age"]\n\n# Clear all\nperson.clear()\nprint(person)  # {}\n\n# Set default - add if doesn\'t exist\ncounts = {}\ncounts.setdefault("a", 0)  # Returns 0, sets counts["a"] = 0\ncounts["a"] += 1</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-6:</strong> Assign to a key to add new or update existing entries.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-12:</strong> <code>update()</code> merges another dictionary or keyword args.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-16:</strong> <code>pop()</code> removes a key and returns its value.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 19-20:</strong> <code>pop()</code> with default won\'t raise KeyError for missing keys.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 23-24:</strong> <code>popitem()</code> removes and returns the last item (LIFO).</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 30-32:</strong> <code>setdefault()</code> sets a value only if the key doesn\'t exist.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Iterating Dictionaries</h2>\n                <p>Loop through dictionaries using various techniques depending on whether you need keys, values, or both.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Dictionary Iteration:</h4>\n<pre><code>person = {"name": "Alice", "age": 25, "city": "NYC"}\n\n# Iterate over keys (default)\nfor key in person:\n    print(key)\n\n# Explicitly iterate keys\nfor key in person.keys():\n    print(key)\n\n# Iterate over values\nfor value in person.values():\n    print(value)\n\n# Iterate over key-value pairs\nfor key, value in person.items():\n    print(f"{key}: {value}")\n\n# Get key and value with index\nfor i, (key, value) in enumerate(person.items()):\n    print(f"{i}. {key} = {value}")\n\n# Build dictionary from two lists\nkeys = ["name", "age", "city"]\nvalues = ["Bob", 30, "LA"]\nperson = dict(zip(keys, values))\nprint(person)  # {\'name\': \'Bob\', \'age\': 30, \'city\': \'LA\'}\n\n# Filter dictionary\nscores = {"Alice": 85, "Bob": 72, "Charlie": 90}\npassed = {k: v for k, v in scores.items() if v >= 75}\nprint(passed)  # {\'Alice\': 85, \'Charlie\': 90}</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-7:</strong> Iterating directly over a dict gives keys.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-14:</strong> <code>.values()</code> lets you iterate over values only.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-18:</strong> <code>.items()</code> gives key-value pairs as tuples.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 21-22:</strong> Unpacking pairs in the loop header is clean and readable.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 31-33:</strong> Dictionary comprehension can filter entries.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Introduction to Sets</h2>\n                <p>Sets are unordered collections of unique elements. They\'re useful for membership testing, removing duplicates, and mathematical operations like union and intersection.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Creating Sets:</h4>\n<pre><code># Empty set (note: {} creates an empty dict!)\nempty = set()\n\n# Set with values\nfruits = {"apple", "banana", "cherry"}\nnumbers = set([1, 2, 2, 3, 3, 3])  # Deduplicates\nprint(numbers)  # {1, 2, 3}\n\n# Set from string\nletters = set("hello")\nprint(letters)  # {\'h\', \'e\', \'l\', \'o\'} - order varies\n\n# Frozenset (immutable set)\nfrozen = frozenset([1, 2, 3])\n# frozen.add(4)  # Error - can\'t modify\n\n# Set comprehension\nsquares = {x**2 for x in range(5)}\nprint(squares)  # {0, 1, 4, 9, 16}\n\n# Sets can only contain immutable (hashable) types\nvalid = {1, "hello", (1, 2)}\n# invalid = {[1, 2]}  # TypeError - lists aren\'t hashable</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Line 2:</strong> Use <code>set()</code> for empty set. <code>{}</code> creates an empty dictionary!</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-7:</strong> Sets automatically remove duplicates.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 10-11:</strong> Sets from iterables keep only unique elements.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 14-16:</strong> <code>frozenset</code> is an immutable, hashable set.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-23:</strong> Sets can only contain hashable (immutable) elements.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Set Operations</h2>\n                <p>Sets support mathematical operations like union, intersection, difference, and more. These are powerful tools for comparing collections.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Mathematical Set Operations:</h4>\n<pre><code>a = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5}\nb = {4, 5, 6, 7, 8}\n\n# Union (all elements from both)\nprint(a | b)        # {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8}\nprint(a.union(b))   # Same result\n\n# Intersection (elements in both)\nprint(a & b)             # {4, 5}\nprint(a.intersection(b)) # Same result\n\n# Difference (in a but not in b)\nprint(a - b)          # {1, 2, 3}\nprint(a.difference(b))# Same result\n\n# Symmetric difference (in either, but not both)\nprint(a ^ b)                   # {1, 2, 3, 6, 7, 8}\nprint(a.symmetric_difference(b)) # Same result\n\n# Subset and superset tests\nc = {1, 2}\nprint(c.issubset(a))     # True\nprint(a.issuperset(c))   # True\nprint(c <= a)            # True (subset operator)\nprint(a >= c)            # True (superset operator)\n\n# Disjoint (no common elements)\nd = {10, 11}\nprint(a.isdisjoint(d))  # True\n\n# Membership testing (very fast!)\nprint(3 in a)  # True\nprint(10 in a) # False</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-6:</strong> Union (<code>|</code>) combines all unique elements from both sets.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-10:</strong> Intersection (<code>&</code>) keeps only elements in both sets.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-14:</strong> Difference (<code>-</code>) keeps elements in left set but not right.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-18:</strong> Symmetric difference (<code>^</code>) keeps elements in either but not both.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 21-25:</strong> Test subset/superset relationships.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 31-32:</strong> Sets provide O(1) membership testing - much faster than lists!</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Modifying Sets</h2>\n                <p>Sets are mutable and support various methods for adding and removing elements.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Set Modification:</h4>\n<pre><code>fruits = {"apple", "banana"}\n\n# Add single element\nfruits.add("cherry")\nprint(fruits)  # {\'apple\', \'banana\', \'cherry\'}\n\n# Add multiple elements\nfruits.update(["date", "elderberry"])\nfruits.update({"fig", "grape"})  # Also works with sets\n\n# Remove element (raises KeyError if missing)\nfruits.remove("apple")\n\n# Discard element (no error if missing)\nfruits.discard("mango")  # No error even though not in set\n\n# Pop random element\nitem = fruits.pop()\nprint(f"Popped: {item}")\n\n# Clear all elements\nfruits.clear()\n\n# Update operations (modify in place)\na = {1, 2, 3}\nb = {3, 4, 5}\n\na.update(b)         # a becomes {1, 2, 3, 4, 5}\na.intersection_update({2, 3, 4})  # a becomes {2, 3, 4}\na.difference_update({2})  # a becomes {3, 4}</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-6:</strong> <code>add()</code> inserts a single element.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-10:</strong> <code>update()</code> adds multiple elements from any iterable.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-14:</strong> <code>remove()</code> raises KeyError if element doesn\'t exist.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-18:</strong> <code>discard()</code> silently ignores missing elements.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 21-22:</strong> <code>pop()</code> removes an arbitrary element (sets are unordered).</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 28-32:</strong> In-place versions of set operations.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Use Cases for Dictionaries and Sets</h2>\n                <p>Here are practical examples showing when to use dictionaries and sets effectively.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Practical Examples:</h4>\n<pre><code># Counting with dictionary\ntext = "the quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog"\nword_count = {}\n\nfor word in text.split():\n    word_count[word] = word_count.get(word, 0) + 1\n\nprint(word_count["the"])  # 2\n\n# Using Counter from collections\nfrom collections import Counter\ncounts = Counter(text.split())\nprint(counts.most_common(2))  # [(\'the\', 2), ...]\n\n# Grouping with dictionary\nfrom collections import defaultdict\nstudents = [("Alice", "A"), ("Bob", "B"), ("Charlie", "A")]\n\ngroups = defaultdict(list)\nfor name, grade in students:\n    groups[grade].append(name)\n\nprint(groups["A"])  # [\'Alice\', \'Charlie\']\n\n# Remove duplicates while preserving order (Python 3.7+)\nitems = [1, 2, 2, 3, 3, 3, 4]\nunique = list(dict.fromkeys(items))\nprint(unique)  # [1, 2, 3, 4]\n\n# Find common elements\nlist1 = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]\nlist2 = [4, 5, 6, 7, 8]\ncommon = list(set(list1) & set(list2))\nprint(common)  # [4, 5]\n\n# Cache/memoization with dictionary\ndef fibonacci(n, cache={}):\n    if n in cache:\n        return cache[n]\n    if n <= 1:\n        return n\n    cache[n] = fibonacci(n-1) + fibonacci(n-2)\n    return cache[n]\n\nprint(fibonacci(50))  # Fast due to caching!</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-9:</strong> Manual counting pattern with dictionary.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 12-14:</strong> <code>Counter</code> is a specialized dictionary for counting.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-22:</strong> <code>defaultdict</code> automatically creates default values for missing keys.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-27:</strong> Deduplicate while preserving order using dict.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 30-32:</strong> Set operations for finding common elements.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 35-42:</strong> Dictionary as cache for memoization.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Practice Exercise</h2>\n                <p>Create a simple contact book using dictionaries.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Exercise Template:</h4>\n<pre><code># Contact book\ncontacts = {}\n\ndef add_contact(name, phone, email):\n    """Add a new contact."""\n    # Your code here\n\ndef get_contact(name):\n    """Get contact info by name."""\n    # Your code here\n\ndef delete_contact(name):\n    """Delete a contact by name."""\n    # Your code here\n\ndef list_contacts():\n    """List all contacts."""\n    # Your code here\n\ndef find_by_phone(phone):\n    """Find contacts by phone number."""\n    # Your code here (hint: iterate through items)\n\n# Test\nadd_contact("Alice", "555-1234", "alice@email.com")\nadd_contact("Bob", "555-5678", "bob@email.com")\nlist_contacts()\nprint(get_contact("Alice"))\ndelete_contact("Bob")\nlist_contacts()</code></pre>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <div class="lesson-navigation">\n                <a href="lesson7.html" class="nav-button prev">← Previous: Lists & Tuples</a>\n                <a href="lesson9.html" class="nav-button next">Next: String Manipulation →</a>\n            </div>\n        </div>\n    </main>\n\n    <footer>\n        <div class="container">\n            <p>&copy; 2024 Learner. Python provides rich functionality for creating, formatting, and manipulating text. Understanding strings deeply will help you work with user input, files, APIs, and more.</p>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>String Creation and Basics</h2>\n                <p>Strings can be created with single, double, or triple quotes. Triple quotes allow multi-line strings.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Creating Strings:</h4>\n<pre><code># Single and double quotes (identical behavior)\nsingle = \'Hello\'\ndouble = "Hello"\nprint(single == double)  # True\n\n# Escape characters\nescaped = "He said \\"Hello\\""\nprint(escaped)  # He said "Hello"\npath = "C:\\\\Users\\\\Documents"  # Backslash escape\nline_break = "Line 1\\nLine 2"  # Newline\n\n# Raw strings (ignore escapes)\nraw_path = r"C:\\Users\\Documents"\nprint(raw_path)  # C:\\Users\\Documents\n\n# Multi-line strings\nmulti = """\nThis is a\nmulti-line string\n"""\n\n# Triple quotes preserve formatting\nhtml = """\n<div class="container">\n    <h1>Title</h1>\n</div>\n"""\n\n# String length\ntext = "Python"\nprint(len(text))  # 6\n\n# Concatenation\nfull = "Hello" + " " + "World"\nrepeated = "Hi" * 3  # "HiHiHi"</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-4:</strong> Single and double quotes work identically in Python.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 7-10:</strong> Use backslash for escape sequences: \\", \\\\, \\n, \\t.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-14:</strong> Raw strings (r"...") treat backslashes literally.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-26:</strong> Triple quotes create multi-line strings preserving whitespace.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 29-33:</strong> Strings support len(), +, and * operations.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>String Indexing and Slicing</h2>\n                <p>Strings are sequences, so you can access individual characters or extract substrings using indexing and slicing.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Accessing String Parts:</h4>\n<pre><code>text = "Python"\n\n# Indexing\nprint(text[0])    # \'P\' (first character)\nprint(text[-1])   # \'n\' (last character)\nprint(text[-2])   # \'o\' (second to last)\n\n# Slicing: [start:end:step]\nprint(text[0:3])  # \'Pyt\' (characters 0, 1, 2)\nprint(text[:3])   # \'Pyt\' (from start to index 2)\nprint(text[2:])   # \'thon\' (from index 2 to end)\nprint(text[::2])  # \'Pto\' (every second character)\n\n# Reverse a string\nprint(text[::-1])  # \'nohtyP\'\n\n# Get last 3 characters\nprint(text[-3:])   # \'hon\'\n\n# Strings are immutable\n# text[0] = \'J\'  # TypeError!\n\n# To "modify", create new string\nnew_text = \'J\' + text[1:]\nprint(new_text)  # \'Jython\'</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-7:</strong> Positive index from start, negative from end (-1 is last).</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 10-13:</strong> Slice syntax [start:end:step]. End is exclusive.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Line 16:</strong> <code>[::-1]</code> is the Pythonic way to reverse a string.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-23:</strong> Strings are immutable - create new strings instead of modifying.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>String Methods</h2>\n                <p>Python strings have many built-in methods for transformation, searching, and testing.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Case Methods:</h4>\n<pre><code>text = "Hello World"\n\n# Case transformations\nprint(text.upper())      # "HELLO WORLD"\nprint(text.lower())      # "hello world"\nprint(text.title())      # "Hello World"\nprint(text.capitalize()) # "Hello world"\nprint(text.swapcase())   # "hELLO wORLD"\n\n# Whitespace handling\nmessy = "  Hello World  "\nprint(messy.strip())     # "Hello World" (both ends)\nprint(messy.lstrip())    # "Hello World  " (left only)\nprint(messy.rstrip())    # "  Hello World" (right only)\n\n# Strip specific characters\nchars = "xxHelloxx"\nprint(chars.strip(\'x\'))  # "Hello"\n\n# Center, left, right align\nprint("Hi".center(10))      # "    Hi    "\nprint("Hi".ljust(10, \'-\'))  # "Hi--------"\nprint("Hi".rjust(10, \'-\'))  # "--------Hi"</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-9:</strong> Various case conversion methods.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 12-17:</strong> <code>strip()</code>, <code>lstrip()</code>, <code>rstrip()</code> remove whitespace.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 20-21:</strong> Can specify characters to strip instead of whitespace.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-26:</strong> Alignment methods for text formatting.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Search and Replace:</h4>\n<pre><code>text = "Hello World World"\n\n# Find substring position\nprint(text.find("World"))     # 6 (first occurrence)\nprint(text.find("Python"))    # -1 (not found)\nprint(text.rfind("World"))    # 12 (last occurrence)\n\n# Find with index (raises error if not found)\nprint(text.index("World"))    # 6\n# text.index("Python")        # ValueError\n\n# Count occurrences\nprint(text.count("World"))    # 2\nprint(text.count("o"))        # 3\n\n# Replace\nprint(text.replace("World", "Python"))\n# "Hello Python Python"\n\n# Replace with count limit\nprint(text.replace("World", "Python", 1))\n# "Hello Python World"\n\n# Check start/end\nprint(text.startswith("Hello"))  # True\nprint(text.endswith("World"))    # True</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-7:</strong> <code>find()</code> returns -1 if not found, <code>rfind()</code> searches from end.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 10-11:</strong> <code>index()</code> is like find but raises ValueError if not found.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 14-15:</strong> <code>count()</code> returns the number of occurrences.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 18-22:</strong> <code>replace()</code> can limit number of replacements.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-26:</strong> Check what string starts or ends with.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Splitting and Joining:</h4>\n<pre><code># Split string into list\nsentence = "Hello World Python"\nwords = sentence.split()  # Default: whitespace\nprint(words)  # [\'Hello\', \'World\', \'Python\']\n\n# Split with delimiter\ncsv = "apple,banana,cherry"\nfruits = csv.split(\',\')\nprint(fruits)  # [\'apple\', \'banana\', \'cherry\']\n\n# Limit splits\ndata = "a:b:c:d"\nprint(data.split(\':\', 2))  # [\'a\', \'b\', \'c:d\']\n\n# Partition (splits into 3 parts)\ntext = "user@example.com"\nbefore, sep, after = text.partition(\'@\')\nprint(before, after)  # user example.com\n\n# Join list into string\nwords = [\'Hello\', \'World\']\nprint(\' \'.join(words))   # "Hello World"\nprint(\'-\'.join(words))   # "Hello-World"\nprint(\'\'.join(words))    # "HelloWorld"\n\n# Join with path separator\nimport os\npaths = [\'folder\', \'subfolder\', \'file.txt\']\nfull_path = os.path.join(*paths)\n\n# Split lines\nmulti = "Line 1\\nLine 2\\nLine 3"\nlines = multi.splitlines()\nprint(lines)  # [\'Line 1\', \'Line 2\', \'Line 3\']</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-5:</strong> <code>split()</code> without argument splits on any whitespace.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 8-10:</strong> Specify delimiter for custom splitting.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-19:</strong> <code>partition()</code> always returns 3 parts: before, separator, after.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-25:</strong> <code>join()</code> is the reverse of split - combines list elements.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 33-35:</strong> <code>splitlines()</code> handles different line ending styles.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>String Testing Methods</h2>\n                <p>Python provides methods to test string content, useful for validation.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Validation Methods:</h4>\n<pre><code># Character type tests\nprint("hello".isalpha())     # True (letters only)\nprint("hello123".isalnum())  # True (letters and numbers)\nprint("12345".isdigit())     # True (digits only)\nprint("123.45".isnumeric())  # False (not purely numeric)\nprint("   ".isspace())       # True (whitespace only)\n\n# Case tests\nprint("hello".islower())     # True\nprint("HELLO".isupper())     # True\nprint("Hello World".istitle())  # True\n\n# Check if printable\nprint("Hello!".isprintable())  # True\nprint("Hello\\n".isprintable()) # False\n\n# Practical validation\ndef validate_username(username):\n    if not username:\n        return False, "Username cannot be empty"\n    if not username.isalnum():\n        return False, "Username must be alphanumeric"\n    if len(username) < 3:\n        return False, "Username too short"\n    return True, "Valid username"\n\nprint(validate_username("user123"))  # (True, "Valid username")\n\n# Check for valid identifier\nprint("my_var".isidentifier())   # True\nprint("123var".isidentifier())   # False (starts with number)</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-7:</strong> Test what characters the string contains.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 10-12:</strong> Check case of string content.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 19-27:</strong> Combining tests for input validation.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 30-31:</strong> <code>isidentifier()</code> checks if string is a valid Python identifier.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>String Formatting</h2>\n                <p>Python offers multiple ways to format strings with dynamic values. F-strings are the modern, recommended approach.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>F-Strings (Python 3.6+):</h4>\n<pre><code>name = "Alice"\nage = 25\nscore = 95.6789\n\n# Basic f-string\nprint(f"Hello, {name}!")\n\n# Expressions inside braces\nprint(f"Next year you\'ll be {age + 1}")\n\n# Format specifications\nprint(f"Score: {score:.2f}")      # "Score: 95.68"\nprint(f"Score: {score:.0f}")      # "Score: 96" (no decimals)\nprint(f"Number: {42:05d}")        # "Number: 00042"\nprint(f"Percent: {0.856:.1%}")    # "Percent: 85.6%"\n\n# Alignment and width\nprint(f"|{name:<10}|")  # |Alice     | (left)\nprint(f"|{name:>10}|")  # |     Alice| (right)\nprint(f"|{name:^10}|")  # |  Alice   | (center)\n\n# Number formatting\nbig_num = 1234567.89\nprint(f"{big_num:,.2f}")   # "1,234,567.89"\nprint(f"{big_num:_,.2f}")  # "1_234_567.89" (underscore separator)\n\n# Date formatting\nfrom datetime import datetime\nnow = datetime.now()\nprint(f"Date: {now:%Y-%m-%d %H:%M}")\n\n# Debug mode (Python 3.8+)\nx = 10\ny = 20\nprint(f"{x=}, {y=}")  # "x=10, y=20"\nprint(f"{x + y =}")   # "x + y =30"</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 7-8:</strong> F-strings evaluate expressions inside curly braces.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-15:</strong> Format specifiers after colon: .Nf for decimals, :Nd for width.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 18-20:</strong> Alignment: < for left, > for right, ^ for center.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 23-25:</strong> Number formatting with comma or underscore separators.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 34-36:</strong> Debug mode shows variable name and value.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Alternative Formatting Methods:</h4>\n<pre><code>name = "Alice"\nage = 25\n\n# format() method\nprint("Hello, {}".format(name))\nprint("Hello, {name}, age {age}".format(name=name, age=age))\n\n# Positional and named arguments\nprint("{0} is {1} years old. {0} likes Python.".format(name, age))\n\n# %-formatting (old style)\nprint("Hello, %s" % name)\nprint("Age: %d, Score: %.2f" % (age, 95.67))\n\n# Template strings (for user-provided format strings)\nfrom string import Template\nt = Template("Hello, $name!")\nprint(t.substitute(name="Alice"))\n\n# Safe substitution (doesn\'t fail on missing)\nt = Template("Hello, $name, your score is $score")\nresult = t.safe_substitute(name="Alice")\nprint(result)  # "Hello, Alice, your score is $score"</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-6:</strong> <code>format()</code> method is flexible but more verbose than f-strings.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Line 9:</strong> Can reference arguments by position multiple times.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 12-13:</strong> %-formatting is older but still seen in legacy code.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 16-22:</strong> Template strings are safer for user-provided format strings.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Regular Expressions</h2>\n                <p>For complex pattern matching, Python\'s re module provides regular expression support.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Pattern Matching:</h4>\n<pre><code>import re\n\ntext = "My phone is 555-1234 and email is user@example.com"\n\n# Search for pattern\nmatch = re.search(r\'\\d{3}-\\d{4}\', text)\nif match:\n    print(match.group())  # "555-1234"\n    print(match.start())  # 12 (start position)\n\n# Find all matches\nemails = re.findall(r\'\\w+@\\w+\\.\\w+\', text)\nprint(emails)  # [\'user@example.com\']\n\n# Find all with groups\ntext = "John: 25, Jane: 30, Bob: 35"\nmatches = re.findall(r\'(\\w+): (\\d+)\', text)\nprint(matches)  # [(\'John\', \'25\'), (\'Jane\', \'30\'), (\'Bob\', \'35\')]\n\n# Replace with pattern\nresult = re.sub(r\'\\d{3}-\\d{4}\', \'XXX-XXXX\', text)\nprint(result)  # Phone number replaced\n\n# Split by pattern\nwords = re.split(r\'[,\\s]+\', "apple, banana, cherry")\nprint(words)  # [\'apple\', \'banana\', \'cherry\']\n\n# Common patterns\npatterns = {\n    \'email\': r\'^[\\w\\.-]+@[\\w\\.-]+\\.\\w+$\',\n    \'phone\': r\'^\\d{3}-\\d{3}-\\d{4}$\',\n    \'url\': r\'^https?://[\\w\\.-]+\\.\\w+\',\n    \'date\': r\'^\\d{4}-\\d{2}-\\d{2}$\'\n}\n\ndef validate(pattern_name, text):\n    return bool(re.match(patterns[pattern_name], text))\n\nprint(validate(\'email\', \'user@example.com\'))  # True</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-8:</strong> <code>re.search()</code> finds first match, returns a Match object.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-12:</strong> <code>re.findall()</code> returns all matches as a list.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-17:</strong> Groups with parentheses return tuples.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 20-21:</strong> <code>re.sub()</code> replaces pattern matches.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-35:</strong> Common regex patterns for validation.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 Regex Tip</h4>\n                    <p>Use raw strings (r"...") for regex patterns to avoid escaping backslashes. Regular expressions are powerful but complex - start simple and test incrementally.</p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Practice Exercise</h2>\n                <p>Create a function that validates and formats a phone number.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Exercise Template:</h4>\n<pre><code>import re\n\ndef format_phone(phone):\n    """\n    Format a phone number to (XXX) XXX-XXXX.\n    Accepts formats: 5551234567, 555-123-4567, \n    (555) 123-4567, etc.\n    \n    Returns formatted string or None if invalid.\n    """\n    # Remove all non-digit characters\n    digits = re.sub(r\'\\D\', \'\', phone)\n    \n    # Check if valid (10 digits)\n    if len(digits) != 10:\n        return None\n    \n    # Format: (XXX) XXX-XXXX\n    # Your code here...\n    return formatted\n\n# Test cases\nprint(format_phone("555-123-4567"))    # (555) 123-4567\nprint(format_phone("(555) 123-4567"))  # (555) 123-4567\nprint(format_phone("5551234567"))      # (555) 123-4567\nprint(format_phone("123-456-789"))     # None (too few digits)\nprint(format_phone("123-456-78901"))   # None (too many digits)</code></pre>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 Solution Hint</h4>\n                    <p>Use string slicing or f-strings: <code>f"({digits[:3]}) {digits[3:6]}-{digits[6:]}"</code></p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <div class="lesson-navigation">\n                <a href="lesson8.html" class="nav-button prev">← Previous: Dictionaries & Sets</a>\n                <a href="lesson10.html" class="nav-button next">Next: File Handling →</a>\n            </div>\n        </div>\n    </main>\n\n    <footer>\n        <div class="container">\n            <p>&copy; 2024 Learner. The <code>with</code> statement ensures files are properly closed, even if errors occur.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>File Opening Basics:</h4>\n<pre><code># Basic file opening\nfile = open("example.txt", "r")  # Open for reading\ncontent = file.read()\nfile.close()  # Always close the file!\n\n# Better: Using \'with\' statement (auto-closes)\nwith open("example.txt", "r") as file:\n    content = file.read()\n# File is automatically closed here\n\n# File modes:\n# \'r\' - read (default)\n# \'w\' - write (overwrites existing)\n# \'a\' - append (adds to end)\n# \'x\' - exclusive creation (fails if exists)\n# \'b\' - binary mode\n# \'t\' - text mode (default)\n# \'+\' - read and write\n\n# Common combinations\nwith open("file.txt", "w") as f:    # Write text\nwith open("file.txt", "a") as f:    # Append text\nwith open("file.bin", "wb") as f:   # Write binary\nwith open("file.txt", "r+") as f:   # Read and write\n\n# Specify encoding for text files\nwith open("file.txt", "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:\n    content = f.read()</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-5:</strong> Manual file opening requires explicit close().</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 8-11:</strong> The <code>with</code> statement automatically closes the file - always prefer this!</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 14-22:</strong> File modes control how the file is opened.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 30-31:</strong> Always specify encoding for text files to avoid issues.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 Always Use \'with\'</h4>\n                    <p>The <code>with</code> statement is called a context manager. It guarantees the file is closed even if an exception occurs, preventing resource leaks and file corruption.</p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Reading Methods:</h4>\n<pre><code># Read entire file as string\nwith open("example.txt", "r") as f:\n    content = f.read()\n    print(content)\n\n# Writing CSV\ndata = [\n    ["Name", "Age", "City"],\n    ["Alice", 25, "NYC"],\n    ["Bob", 30, "LA"],\n    ["Charlie", 35, "Chicago"]\n]\n\nwith open("people.csv", "w", newline="") as f:\n    writer = csv.writer(f)\n    writer.writerows(data)\n\n# Reading CSV\nwith open("people.csv", "r") as f:\n    reader = csv.reader(f)\n    for row in reader:\n        print(row)\n\n# CSV with "city": "NYC"})\n\n# Custom delimiter\nwith open("data.tsv", "w", newline="") as f:\n    writer = csv.writer(f, delimiter="\\t")\n    writer.writerows(data)</code></pre>\n                    <div           <p>The pathlib module provides an object-oriented approach to working with file paths.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Using            <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Binary Operations:</h4>\n<pre><code># Read binary file\nwith open("image.png", "rb") as f:\n    data = f.read()\n    print(len(data))  # Python is a multi-paradigm language that fully supports OOP. OOP helps create modular, reusable, and maintainable code by modeling real-world entities as objects.</p>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Classes and Objects</h2>\n                <p>A class is a blueprint for creating objects. An object is an instance of a class with specific attribute values.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Creating Classes:</h4>\n<pre><code># Basic class definition\nclass Dog:\n    """A simple Dog class."""\n    \n    # Class attribute (shared by all instances)\n    species = "Canis familiaris"\n    \n    # Constructor (initializer)\n    def __init__(self, name, age):\n        # Instance attributes (unique to each instance)\n        self.name = name\n        self.age = age\n    \n    # Instance method\n    def bark(self):\n        return f"{self.name} says Woof!"\n    \n    # Method with parameter\n    def birthday(self):\n        self.age += 1\n        return f"{self.name} is now {age}!"\n\n# Create objects (instances)\ndog1 = Dog("Buddy", 3)\ndog2 = Dog("Max", 5)\n\n# Access attributes\nprint(dog1.name)        # Buddy\nprint(dog2.age)         # 5\nprint(dog1.species)     # Canis familiaris\n\n# Call methods\nprint(dog1.bark())      # Buddy says Woof!\n\n# Modify attributes\ndog1.age = 4\nprint(dog1.age)         # 4\n\n# Check class and instance\nprint(type(dog1))       # <class \'__main__.Dog\'>\nprint(isinstance(dog1, Dog))  # True</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-4:</strong> Use <code>class</code> keyword. Docstrings describe the class.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 7-8:</strong> Class attributes are shared by all instances of the class.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-14:</strong> <code>__init__</code> is the constructor. <code>self</code> refers to the instance being created.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-18:</strong> Instance methods take <code>self</code> as the first parameter.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-25:</strong> Create objects by calling the class like a function.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 28-30:</strong> Access attributes using dot notation.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 Understanding \'self\'</h4>\n                    <p><code>self</code> is a convention (not a keyword) that refers to the current instance. It\'s automatically passed as the first argument to instance methods. You can name it differently, but <code>self</code> is the standard convention.</p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Class Attributes vs Instance Attributes</h2>\n                <p>Understanding the difference between class and instance attributes is crucial for proper OOP design.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Attribute Types:</h4>\n<pre><code>class Counter:\n    # Class attribute - shared by all instances\n    total_count = 0\n    \n    def __init__(self, name):\n        # Instance attribute - unique to each instance\n        self.name = name\n        self.count = 0\n        # Modify class attribute\n        Counter.total_count += 1\n    \n    def increment(self):\n        self.count += 1\n    \n    @classmethod\n    def get_total(cls):\n        """Class method - works with class, not instance."""\n        return cls.total_count\n\n# Create instances\nc1 = Counter("First")\nc2 = Counter("Second")\n\nprint(Counter.total_count)  # 2 (class attribute)\nprint(c1.count)             # 0 (instance attribute)\nprint(c2.count)             # 0 (instance attribute)\n\nc1.increment()\nprint(c1.count)             # 1\nprint(c2.count)             # 0 (unchanged)\n\n# Be careful with mutable class attributes!\nclass Buggy:\n    items = []  # Shared by all instances!\n\na = Buggy()\nb = Buggy()\na.items.append("x")\nprint(b.items)  # [\'x\'] - Not what you might expect!\n\n# Better approach\nclass Fixed:\n    def __init__(self):\n        self.items = []  # Each instance has its own list</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-4:</strong> Class attributes are defined outside methods and shared.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 7-10:</strong> Instance attributes are defined in <code>__init__</code> with <code>self</code>.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-17:</strong> <code>@classmethod</code> creates methods that work with the class.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 28-31:</strong> Modifying one instance doesn\'t affect others\' instance attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 34-42:</strong> Mutable class attributes can cause unexpected sharing - avoid them!</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Special Methods (Dunder Methods)</h2>\n                <p>Special methods with double underscores (dunder methods) let you define how objects behave with built-in operations.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Common Special Methods:</h4>\n<pre><code>class Vector:\n    def __init__(self, x, y):\n        self.x = x\n        self.y = y\n    \n    # String representation (for users)\n    def __str__(self):\n        return f"Vector({self.x}, {self.y})"\n    \n    # Representation (for developers)\n    def __repr__(self):\n        return f"Vector({self.x}, {self.y})"\n    \n    # Addition: v1 + v2\n    def __add__(self, other):\n        return Vector(self.x + other.x, self.y + other.y)\n    \n    # Subtraction: v1 - v2\n    def __sub__(self, other):\n        return Vector(self.x - other.x, self.y - other.y)\n    \n    # Multiplication: v * scalar\n    def __mul__(self, scalar):\n        return Vector(self.x * scalar, self.y * scalar)\n    \n    # Length: len(v)\n    def __len__(self):\n        return int((self.x**2 + self.y**2) ** 0.5)\n    \n    # Equality: v1 == v2\n    def __eq__(self, other):\n        return self.x == other.x and self.y == other.y\n    \n    # Hash for use in sets and dicts\n    def __hash__(self):\n        return hash((self.x, self.y))\n    \n    # Boolean conversion: bool(v)\n    def __bool__(self):\n        return self.x != 0 or self.y != 0\n\n# Usage\nv1 = Vector(3, 4)\nv2 = Vector(1, 2)\n\nprint(v1)           # Vector(3, 4) - uses __str__\nprint(v1 + v2)      # Vector(4, 6)\nprint(v1 * 2)       # Vector(6, 8)\nprint(len(v1))      # 5 (magnitude)\nprint(v1 == v2)     # False</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 8-9:</strong> <code>__str__</code> defines how the object appears in print().</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 12-13:</strong> <code>__repr__</code> should return a valid Python expression.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 16-18:</strong> <code>__add__</code> enables the <code>+</code> operator.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-27:</strong> <code>__mul__</code> enables multiplication.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 36-37:</strong> <code>__eq__</code> defines equality comparison.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 48-53:</strong> These special methods make custom objects work naturally with Python syntax.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Inheritance</h2>\n                <p>Inheritance allows a class to inherit attributes and methods from another class. This promotes code reuse and establishes relationships between classes.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Class Inheritance:</h4>\n<pre><code># Base class (parent)\nclass Animal:\n    def __init__(self, name):\n        self.name = name\n    \n    def speak(self):\n        raise NotImplementedError("Subclass must implement")\n    \n    def info(self):\n        return f"I am {self.name}"\n\n# Derived class (child)\nclass Dog(Animal):\n    def __init__(self, name, breed):\n        # Call parent constructor\n        super().__init__(name)\n        self.breed = breed\n    \n    # Override parent method\n    def speak(self):\n        return f"{self.name} says Woof!"\n    \n    # Add new method\n    def fetch(self):\n        return f"{self.name} fetches the ball!"\n\nclass Cat(Animal):\n    def speak(self):\n        return f"{self.name} says Meow!"\n\n# Create instances\ndog = Dog("Buddy", "Golden Retriever")\ncat = Cat("Whiskers")\n\nprint(dog.speak())      # Buddy says Woof!\nprint(cat.speak())      # Whiskers says Meow!\nprint(dog.info())       # I am Buddy (inherited)\nprint(dog.breed)        # Golden Retriever\n\n# Check inheritance\nprint(isinstance(dog, Dog))     # True\nprint(isinstance(dog, Animal))  # True\nprint(issubclass(Dog, Animal))  # True\n\n# Multiple inheritance\nclass Flying:\n    def fly(self):\n        return "I can fly!"\n\nclass Swimming:\n    def swim(self):\n        return "I can swim!"\n\nclass Duck(Animal, Flying, Swimming):\n    def speak(self):\n        return f"{self.name} says Quack!"\n\ndonald = Duck("Donald")\nprint(donald.fly())   # I can fly!\nprint(donald.swim())  # I can swim!</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-10:</strong> Base class defines common interface. Abstract methods raise NotImplementedError.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-22:</strong> Child class inherits from parent (in parentheses). Use <code>super()</code> to call parent methods.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 17-18:</strong> Override parent methods by redefining them.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 37-39:</strong> <code>isinstance()</code> and <code>issubclass()</code> check inheritance relationships.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 42-57:</strong> Python supports multiple inheritance - class inherits from multiple parents.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Encapsulation and Access Control</h2>\n                <p>Encapsulation hides internal details and protects data. Python uses conventions rather than strict access control.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Access Control:</h4>\n<pre><code>class BankAccount:\n    def __init__(self, owner, balance):\n        self.owner = owner           # Public\n        self._balance = balance      # Protected (convention)\n        self.__id = id(self)         # Name mangled (private-ish)\n    \n    # Public method\n    def deposit(self, amount):\n        if amount > 0:\n            self._balance += amount\n            return True\n        return False\n    \n    def withdraw(self, amount):\n        if 0 < amount <= self._balance:\n            self._balance -= amount\n            return True\n        return False\n    \n    # Property for controlled access\n    @property\n    def balance(self):\n        """Read-only access to balance."""\n        return self._balance\n    \n    # Setter with validation\n    @balance.setter\n    def balance(self, value):\n        if value >= 0:\n            self._balance = value\n        else:\n            raise ValueError("Balance cannot be negative")\n\n# Usage\naccount = BankAccount("Alice", 1000)\n\nprint(account.owner)       # Alice (public)\nprint(account.balance)     # 1000 (via property)\n\n# Can access "protected" (but shouldn\'t)\nprint(account._balance)    # 1000 (accessible but discouraged)\n\n# Name mangled attribute\n# print(account.__id)      # AttributeError\nprint(account._BankAccount__id)  # Accessible but very discouraged\n\n# Use methods for operations\naccount.deposit(500)\nprint(account.balance)     # 1500</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-6:</strong> Public (no prefix), protected (<code>_</code>), private (<code>__</code>).</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-19:</strong> Public methods provide controlled access to internal data.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-24:</strong> <code>@property</code> creates read-only attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 27-31:</strong> Property setters allow validation on assignment.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 38-39:</strong> Protected attributes can be accessed but convention says don\'t.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 42-43:</strong> Name mangling makes private attributes harder to access.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 Python\'s Philosophy</h4>\n                    <p>Python trusts developers. Unlike other languages, it doesn\'t enforce strict access control. The convention is: use a single underscore for "internal use" and double underscore for name mangling. True privacy doesn\'t exist in Python!</p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Class Methods and Static Methods</h2>\n                <p>Besides instance methods, Python supports class methods (that receive the class) and static methods (that receive neither class nor instance).</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Method Types:</h4>\n<pre><code>class Person:\n    population = 0\n    \n    def __init__(self, name):\n        self.name = name\n        Person.population += 1\n    \n    # Instance method - receives self\n    def greet(self):\n        return f"Hello, I\'m {self.name}"\n    \n    # Class method - receives cls\n    @classmethod\n    def from_string(cls, string):\n        """Alternative constructor from string."""\n        name = string.split("-")[0]\n        return cls(name)\n    \n    @classmethod\n    def get_population(cls):\n        return f"Population: {cls.population}"\n    \n    # Static method - receives nothing\n    @staticmethod\n    def is_valid_name(name):\n        """Utility function related to Person."""\n        return len(name) >= 2 and name.isalpha()\n\n# Instance method\np1 = Person("Alice")\nprint(p1.greet())  # Hello, I\'m Alice\n\n# Class method as alternative constructor\np2 = Person.from_string("Bob-Smith")\nprint(p2.name)     # Bob\n\n# Class method on class or instance\nprint(Person.get_population())  # Population: 2\nprint(p1.get_population())      # Also works\n\n# Static method - no self or cls needed\nprint(Person.is_valid_name("Alice"))  # True\nprint(Person.is_valid_name("A"))      # False</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-10:</strong> Instance methods take <code>self</code> and can access instance attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-18:</strong> <code>@classmethod</code> methods take <code>cls</code> and can access class attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-17:</strong> Class methods are often used as alternative constructors.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-26:</strong> <code>@staticmethod</code> methods don\'t take <code>self</code> or <code>cls</code>.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 32-33:</strong> Static methods are utility functions logically related to the class.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Data Classes</h2>\n                <p>Python 3.7+ introduced data classes, which automatically generate common methods like <code>__init__</code>, <code>__repr__</code>, and <code>__eq__</code>.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Data Classes:</h4>\n<pre><code>from dataclasses import dataclass, field\nfrom typing import List\n\n@dataclass\nclass Product:\n    name: str\n    price: float\n    quantity: int = 1\n    tags: List[str] = field(default_factory=list)\n    \n    def total_cost(self) -> float:\n        return self.price * self.quantity\n\n# Create instances\np1 = Product("Widget", 9.99)\np2 = Product("Gadget", 19.99, quantity=3)\n\nprint(p1)  # Product(name=\'Widget\', price=9.99, quantity=1, tags=[])\n\n# Auto-generated __eq__\nprint(p1 == Product("Widget", 9.99))  # True\n\n# Access methods\nprint(p2.total_cost())  # 59.97\n\n# Frozen dataclass (immutable)\n@dataclass(frozen=True)\nclass Point:\n    x: float\n    y: float\n\np = Point(3.0, 4.0)\n# p.x = 5.0  # FrozenInstanceError\n\n# Post-init processing\n@dataclass\nclass Rectangle:\n    width: float\n    height: float\n    area: float = field(init=False)\n    \n    def __post_init__(self):\n        self.area = self.width * self.height\n\nr = Rectangle(5, 3)\nprint(r.area)  # 15.0</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-8:</strong> Type annotations define attributes. Default values are supported.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Line 8:</strong> Use <code>field(default_factory=list)</code> for mutable defaults.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 10-11:</strong> Data classes can have regular methods too.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-26:</strong> <code>frozen=True</code> makes the dataclass immutable.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 29-37:</strong> <code>__post_init__</code> runs after initialization for computed fields.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Practice Exercise</h2>\n                <p>Create a Library system with Book and Member classes.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Exercise Template:</h4>\n<pre><code>from dataclasses import dataclass\nfrom typing import Optional\nfrom datetime import date\n\n@dataclass\nclass Book:\n    title: str\n    author: str\n    isbn: str\n    available: bool = True\n\nclass Member:\n    def __init__(self, name: str, member_id: str):\n        self.name = name\n        self.member_id = member_id\n        self.borrowed_books: list[Book] = []\n    \n    def borrow_book(self, book: Book) -> bool:\n        """Borrow a book if available."""\n        # Python uses exceptions to signal that something unexpected happened. Proper error handling makes your programs robust and user-friendly by gracefully handling problems instead of crashing.</p>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Try-Except Basics</h2>\n                <p>The try-except block lets you catch and handle exceptions. Code that might raise an exception goes in the try block, and handling code goes in except blocks.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Basic Exception Handling:</h4>\n<pre><code># Basic try-except\ntry:\n    number = int(input("Enter a number: "))\n    print(f"You entered: {number}")\nexcept ValueError:\n    print("That\'s not a valid number!")\n\n# Catching multiple exceptions\ntry:\n    value = int(input("Enter a number: "))\n    result = 10 / value\n    print(f"Result: {result}")\nexcept ValueError:\n    print("Invalid number!")\nexcept ZeroDivisionError:\n    print("Cannot divide by zero!")\n\n# Catching multiple exceptions together\ntry:\n    file = open("nonexistent.txt", "r")\n    content = file.read()\nexcept (FileNotFoundError, PermissionError) as e:\n    print(f"Error: {e}")\n\n# Catch all exceptions (use sparingly!)\ntry:\n    risky_operation()\nexcept Exception as e:\n    print(f"An error occurred: {e}")\n    # Better to catch specific exceptions\n\n# The \'as\' keyword captures the exception object\ntry:\n    result = 10 / 0\nexcept ZeroDivisionError as error:\n    print(f"Error: {error}")\n    print(f"Error type: {type(error).__name__}")</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-6:</strong> The try block contains code that might fail. Except catches specific exceptions.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 9-16:</strong> Multiple except blocks handle different types of exceptions.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 19-22:</strong> Group related exceptions in a tuple to handle them the same way.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-28:</strong> Catching Exception catches everything but should be used sparingly.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 31-35:</strong> The <code>as</code> keyword captures the exception object for inspection.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>The Full Try-Except Structure</h2>\n                <p>Python\'s try statement can include else and finally blocks for complete control over execution flow.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Complete Try Statement:</h4>\n<pre><code>def divide_numbers(a, b):\n    try:\n        result = a / b\n    except ZeroDivisionError:\n        print("Cannot divide by zero!")\n        return None\n    except TypeError:\n        print("Both arguments must be numbers!")\n        return None\n    else:\n        # Runs if no exception occurred\n        print("Division successful!")\n        return result\n    finally:\n        # Always runs, regardless of exceptions\n        print("Operation complete.")\n\n# Test cases\nprint(divide_numbers(10, 2))\n# Division successful.\n# Operation complete.\n# 5.0\n\nprint(divide_numbers(10, 0))\n# Cannot divide by zero!\n# Operation complete.\n# None\n\n# Practical example with file handling\ndef read_config(filename):\n    config = {}\n    try:\n        with open(filename) as f:\n            config = parse_config(f)\n    except FileNotFoundError:\n        print(f"Config file not found: {filename}")\n    except PermissionError:\n        print(f"Permission denied: {filename}")\n    else:\n        print("Config loaded successfully")\n    finally:\n        print("Config loading complete")\n    return config\n\n# While-else and for-else with exceptions\ndef find_item(items, target):\n    for item in items:\n        if item == target:\n            return item\n    else:\n        # Runs if loop completed without finding\n        raise ValueError(f"{target} not found in items")</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 4-12:</strong> The else block runs only if no exception occurred.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-15:</strong> The finally block always runs, used for cleanup.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 22-28:</strong> Even with early return, finally still executes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 31-42:</strong> Real-world pattern for file handling with multiple exception types.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 45-52:</strong> The else clause in loops runs if no break occurred.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n\n                <div class="tip-box">\n                    <h4>💡 When to Use Each Block</h4>\n                    <p>Use <strong>try</strong> for risky code, <strong>except</strong> for handling errors, <strong>else</strong> for code that runs on success, and <strong>finally</strong> for cleanup that must always happen (closing files, releasing resources).</p>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Raising Exceptions</h2>\n                <p>You can raise exceptions using the <code>raise</code> keyword. This is useful for validating inputs or signaling error conditions in your code.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Raising Exceptions:</h4>\n<pre><code># Raise a simple exception\ndef set_age(age):\n    if age < 0:\n        raise ValueError("Age cannot be negative")\n    return age\n\ntry:\n    set_age(-5)\nexcept ValueError as e:\n    print(e)  # Age cannot be negative\n\n# Re-raising exceptions\ndef process_data(data):\n    try:\n        result = parse(data)\n    except ValueError:\n        print("Logging error...")\n        raise  # Re-raise the same exception\n\n# Raise with custom message\ndef withdraw(balance, amount):\n    if amount > balance:\n        raise ValueError(\n            f"Insufficient funds: {balance} available, "\n            f"{amount} requested"\n        )\n    return balance - amount\n\n# Raise different exception types\ndef validate_user(user):\n    if not user:\n        raise TypeError("User cannot be None")\n    if not user.get("name"):\n        raise ValueError("User must have a name")\n    if user.get("age", 0) < 0:\n        raise ValueError("Age cannot be negative")\n\n# Chaining exceptions (Python 3+)\ntry:\n    try:\n        value = int("not a number")\n    except ValueError as e:\n        raise RuntimeError("Processing failed") from e\n\n# Exception chaining shows the original cause\n# RuntimeError: Processing failed\n# The above exception was the direct cause of...</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-8:</strong> Use <code>raise</code> to signal error conditions in your functions.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-15:</strong> Use bare <code>raise</code> to re-raise the current exception after logging.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 18-24:</strong> Provide descriptive error messages with context.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 27-33:</strong> Raise different exception types for different error conditions.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 36-42:</strong> Use <code>raise ... from e</code> to chain exceptions and preserve context.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Custom Exceptions</h2>\n                <p>Create your own exception classes for domain-specific errors. Custom exceptions make your code more expressive and easier to handle.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Creating Custom Exceptions:</h4>\n<pre><code># Basic custom exception\nclass InsufficientFundsError(Exception):\n    """Raised when account has insufficient funds."""\n    pass\n\n# Custom exception with attributes\nclass BankError(Exception):\n    """Base exception for bank operations."""\n    def __init__(self, message, code=None):\n        self.message = message\n        self.code = code\n        super().__init__(self.message)\n\nclass InsufficientFundsError(BankError):\n    """Raised when withdrawal exceeds balance."""\n    def __init__(self, balance, amount):\n        self.balance = balance\n        self.amount = amount\n        super().__init__(\n            f"Insufficient funds: {balance} available, "\n            f"{amount} requested",\n            code="INSUFFICIENT_FUNDS"\n        )\n\n# Using custom exceptions\nclass BankAccount:\n    def __init__(self, balance=0):\n        self.balance = balance\n    \n    def withdraw(self, amount):\n        if amount > self.balance:\n            raise InsufficientFundsError(self.balance, amount)\n        self.balance -= amount\n        return amount\n\n# Handling custom exceptions\ntry:\n    account = BankAccount(100)\n    account.withdraw(150)\nexcept InsufficientFundsError as e:\n    print(f"Error: {e}")\n    print(f"Balance: {e.balance}")\n    print(f"Code: {e.code}")\n\n# Exception hierarchy\nclass AppError(Exception):\n    """Base exception for the application."""\n\nclass DatabaseError(AppError):\n    """Database-related errors."""\n\nclass ConnectionError(DatabaseError):\n    """Connection failures."""\n\nclass QueryError(DatabaseError):\n    """Query execution failures."""\n\n# Catch by hierarchy\ntry:\n    raise ConnectionError("Failed to connect")\nexcept DatabaseError:  # Catches any DatabaseError subclass\n    print("Database operation failed")</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-5:</strong> Simple custom exception inheriting from Exception.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 8-12:</strong> Custom exception with additional attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-24:</strong> Specialized exception with contextual attributes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 27-34:</strong> Use custom exceptions in your classes.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 37-41:</strong> Access custom attributes when handling the exception.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 44-57:</strong> Create a hierarchy of exceptions for organized error handling.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Common Built-in Exceptions</h2>\n                <p>Python has many built-in exceptions. Understanding them helps you write better error handling code.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Common Exceptions:</h4>\n<pre><code># ValueError - right type, inappropriate value\nint("abc")  # ValueError\n\n# TypeError - wrong type for operation\n"hello" + 5  # TypeError\n\n# IndexError - list index out of range\n[1, 2, 3][10]  # IndexError\n\n# KeyError - dictionary key not found\n{"a": 1}["b"]  # KeyError\n\n# FileNotFoundError - file doesn\'t exist\nopen("missing.txt")  # FileNotFoundError\n\n# ZeroDivisionError - division by zero\n10 / 0  # ZeroDivisionError\n\n# AttributeError - object lacks attribute\n"hello".unknown_method()  # AttributeError\n\n# NameError - variable not defined\nprint(undefined_var)  # NameError\n\n# Handle specific exceptions appropriately\ndef safe_get(dictionary, key, default=None):\n    try:\n        return dictionary[key]\n    except KeyError:\n        return default\n\ndef safe_divide(a, b):\n    try:\n        return a / b\n    except ZeroDivisionError:\n        return float(\'inf\')\n\ndef safe_index(lst, index):\n    try:\n        return lst[index]\n    except IndexError:\n        return None</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-3:</strong> ValueError occurs when value is inappropriate for the operation.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 5-6:</strong> TypeError occurs when types are incompatible.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 8-9:</strong> IndexError occurs when accessing non-existent list index.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 11-12:</strong> KeyError occurs when dictionary key doesn\'t exist.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 20-25:</strong> Create safe wrapper functions for common operations.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Context Managers</h2>\n                <p>Context managers handle setup and cleanup automatically using the <code>with</code> statement. They\'re perfect for resource management like files, connections, and locks.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Creating Context Managers:</h4>\n<pre><code># Using context manager with files\nwith open("file.txt", "w") as f:\n    f.write("Hello")\n# File automatically closed\n\n# Creating context manager with class\nclass Timer:\n    def __enter__(self):\n        import time\n        self.start = time.time()\n        return self\n    \n    def __exit__(self, exc_type, exc_val, exc_tb):\n        self.end = time.time()\n        self.elapsed = self.end - self.start\n        print(f"Elapsed time: {self.elapsed:.2f}s")\n        return False  # Don\'t suppress exceptions\n\n# Using the custom context manager\nwith Timer() as t:\n    sum(range(1000000))\n# Elapsed time: 0.05s\n\n# Creating context manager with decorator\nfrom contextlib import contextmanager\n\n@contextmanager\ndef open_file(filename, mode="r"):\n    f = open(filename, mode)\n    try:\n        yield f\n    finally:\n        f.close()\n\nwith open_file("file.txt") as f:\n    content = f.read()\n\n# Database connection context manager\n@contextmanager\ndef database_connection(host):\n    conn = connect_to_db(host)\n    try:\n        yield conn\n    finally:\n        conn.close()\n\n# Suppress specific exceptions\nfrom contextlib import suppress\n\nwith suppress(FileNotFoundError):\n    os.remove("file.txt")  # Won\'t raise if file doesn\'t exist</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 2-4:</strong> Files are the most common use of context managers.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 7-17:</strong> Class-based context manager with <code>__enter__</code> and <code>__exit__</code>.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 15-16:</strong> <code>__exit__</code> receives exception info; return True to suppress.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 24-32:</strong> Use <code>@contextmanager</code> decorator for simpler creation.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Line 28:</strong> <code>yield</code> provides the value to the <code>as</code> variable.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 43-45:</strong> <code>suppress</code> context manager ignores specific exceptions.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Best Practices</h2>\n                <p>Follow these best practices for effective error handling.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Error Handling Guidelines:</h4>\n<pre><code># 1. Be specific about exceptions\n# Bad\ntry:\n    do_something()\nexcept:  # Catches everything, including KeyboardInterrupt\n    pass\n\n# Good\ntry:\n    do_something()\nexcept (ValueError, TypeError) as e:\n    handle_error(e)\n\n# 2. Don\'t use exceptions for flow control\n# Bad\ntry:\n    item = my_list[index]\nexcept IndexError:\n    item = None\n\n# Good\nif index < len(my_list):\n    item = my_list[index]\nelse:\n    item = None\n\n# 3. Clean up resources properly\n# Good - use context managers\nwith open("file.txt") as f:\n    process(f)\n\n# 4. Provide helpful error messages\n# Bad\nif value < 0:\n    raise ValueError("Invalid value")\n\n# Good\nif value < 0:\n    raise ValueError(\n        f"Value must be non-negative, got {value}"\n    )\n\n# 5. Log exceptions before re-raising\nimport logging\nlogger = logging.getLogger(__name__)\n\ndef process(data):\n    try:\n        return transform(data)\n    except ValueError as e:\n        logger.error(f"Processing failed: {e}")\n        raise\n\n# 6. Use custom exceptions for domain errors\nclass ValidationError(Exception):\n    """Input validation failed."""\n    pass</code></pre>\n                    <div class="code-explanation">\n                        <p><strong>Lines 3-10:</strong> Never use bare <code>except:</code> - it catches system exceptions too.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 13-22:</strong> Use conditional checks instead of exceptions for expected conditions.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 25-27:</strong> Always clean up resources with context managers.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 30-38:</strong> Include relevant information in error messages.</p>\n                        <p><strong>Lines 41-48:</strong> Log exceptions for debugging before re-raising.</p>\n                    </div>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <section class="content-section">\n                <h2>Practice Exercise</h2>\n                <p>Create a simple calculator with robust error handling.</p>\n                \n                <div class="code-block">\n                    <h4>Exercise Template:</h4>\n<pre><code>class CalculatorError(Exception):\n    """Base exception for calculator errors."""\n    pass\n\nclass InvalidOperationError(CalculatorError):\n    """Invalid operation requested."""\n    pass\n\nclass DivisionByZeroError(CalculatorError):\n    """Attempted division by zero."""\n    pass\n\ndef calculate(a: float, b: float, operation: str) -> float:\n    """\n    Perform a calculation on two numbers.\n    \n    Args:\n        a: First number\n        b: Second number\n        operation: One of \'+\', \'-\', \'*\', \'/\'\n    \n    Returns:\n        Result of the calculation\n    \n    Raises:\n        InvalidOperationError: If operation is not supported\n        DivisionByZeroError: If dividing by zero\n    """\n    # Your code here\n    # Use a dictionary of operations or if-elif-else\n    pass\n\ndef safe_calculate(a, b, operation):\n    """Wrapper that handles errors and returns None on failure."""\n    try:\n        return calculate(a, b, operation)\n    except CalculatorError as e:\n        print(f"Error: {e}")\n        return None\n\n# Test\nprint(safe_calculate(10, 5, "+"))   # 15\nprint(safe_calculate(10, 0, "/"))   # Error: Division by zero\nprint(safe_calculate(10, 5, "^"))   # Error: Invalid operation</code></pre>\n                </div>\n            </section>\n\n            <div class="lesson-navigation">\n                <a href="lesson11.html" class="nav-button prev">← Previous: Object-Oriented Programming</a>\n                <a href="lesson13.html" class="nav-button next">Next: Modules & Packages →</a>\n            </div>\n        </div>\n    </main>\n\n    <footer>\n        <div class="container">\n            <p>&copy; 2024 Learner.
